EACH MAN'S SON

The dust jacket of the first edition published in Toronto by the Macmillan Co. of Canada in 1951. Consisting of orange and gray-brown panels it is identical (except for the colours used) to the first American edition published by Little, Brown and Co. of Boston in 1951. The back panel of the Canadian dust jacket is a carefully posed photograph of MacLennan from the studios of the then-famous photographer, Yusuf Karsh of Ottawa.

EACH MAN'S SON

Author's Note

The first paperback edition published in 1962 in the New Canadian Library Series (as number 30) with an introduction by Alec Lucas. The cover graphic is a strong line drawing of a man's face suggestive of MacLennan himself.
GENERAL EDITOR'S NOTE

The text of _Each Man's Son_ presented here was derived by means of an optical character recognition scan of the first edition published by Macmillan Co. of Canada, Toronto, and Little, Brown and Company of Boston, Massachusetts in 1951. Minor changes have been effected to correct typographical errors and/or spelling errors. Canadian spelling is used for this edition.

A paperback edition issued by the Macmillan Co. of Canada in 1971 as number 11 in its Laurentian Library Series. The cover image represents the three main characters of the novel.
A HUGH MACLENNAN CHRONOLOGY

1907 John Hugh MacLennan born on 20 March in Glace Bay, Nova Scotia, son and second child of Katherine MacQuarrie and Samuel MacLennan, a medical doctor employed by the coal mining industry.

1915 The MacLennan household moves from Cape Breton Island to Halifax where Dr. MacLennan sets up his practice after having been invalided out of service in the First World War.

1917 MacLennan lives through the catastrophe of the Halifax Explosion which will become the subject of his first published novel, _Barometer Rising_ (1941).

1924 MacLennan graduates from Halifax County Academy earning the distinction of a University Entrance Scholarship and the Yeoman Prize in Latin and Greek. Enrols in Dalhousie University to study classics, at which he excels.

1928 Having distinguished himself academically and in sports MacLennan graduates from Dalhousie University, suffers a disappointment at not winning the (expected and hoped for) Rhodes Scholarship for Nova Scotia but is then chosen Rhodes Scholar for Canada-at-Large. Sails for England and Oxford in September of that year.

1928–32 Once settled in at Oriel College MacLennan found his studies in the classics rigorous and demanding. He had little social life other than sports (rugger and tennis, he was a champion player at the university); wrote frequent letters to his family, and took advantage of his vacations to travel modestly on the continent in France, Italy, Austria, and Germany, where he encountered strong right-wing nationalism and the rising tides of fascism and communism. He earned his BA in classics from Oxford in 1932 and won a fellowship at Princeton to continue his studies for a PhD.

1932 Sailing home from Oxford MacLennan met on board ship an American woman, Dorothy Duncan (1903–1957), who would become a writer herself ( _Bluenose: A Portrait of Nova Scotia_ , 1942) as well as a graphic artist, and would marry MacLennan four years later.

1932–1935 Unable to find suitable employment MacLennan, who had been offered a modest fellowship at Princeton, decided to study there for his doctorate in classical history. While at the university MacLennan tries his hand at writing fiction which will result in two early novels – "So All Their Praises" and "A Man Should Rejoice" – both of which will remain unpublished, although the first novel dated 1933 was accepted by a New York publisher, Robert O. Ballou, a firm that failed before the novel could be published. In 1935 MacLennan receives his doctorate and his dissertation, _Oxyrhynchus: An Economic and Social Study_ , is published by Princeton University Press. He moves to Montreal and takes up a position as schoolmaster at Lower Canada College.

1936 Hugh MacLennan and Dorothy Duncan are married on 22 June in Wilmette, Illinois, returning via Boston and Yarmouth, Nova Scotia, to settle in Montreal.

1937–1939 MacLennan struggles unsuccessfully to arrange for the publication of his two novels in manuscript, "A Man Should Rejoice," which is rejected by Random House and "So All Their Praises," which is rejected by Longmans Green, both of New York. In 1937 he undertakes a trip to the Soviet Union from which he returns disillusioned. He reportedly reads Ringuet's _Trente Arpents_ , which had appeared in 1938 and without which MacLennan later confessed _Two Solitudes_ could not have been written. Predicts that war will begin in September of 1939.

1936–1941 MacLennan continues at Lower Canada College, begins to develop a career at writing for magazines, and is prompted by his wife to turn his interests in fiction to Canadian themes. She, having written a guidebook, _Here's to Canada!_ (1941), is herself embarked on her book _Bluenose_ (1942), and is credited with having MacLennan fix on the Halifax explosion of 1917 (through which he had lived) as the focus of his novel _Barometer Rising_ with which his career as novelist will be truly launched.

1941 _Barometer Rising_ is published and is well-received. MacLennan continues to teach at Lower Canada College, but feels financially constrained as he endeavours to supplement his income as a magazine writer.

1943 MacLennan is awarded a Guggenheim Fellowship, which provides some financial relief and frees him somewhat to work on his next novel, _Two Solitudes_ , the beginnings of which he had signaled the previous year. MacLennan's declared project is to write a novel of Canadian life in the years 1917–1940. Announcement of the fellowship generates a series of letters from American publishers (J.B. Lippincott; Doubleday, Doran; Houghton, Mifflin) expressing an interest in publishing his next novel.

1944 MacLennan begins correspondence with Willem L. Graff, a professor of German, as he tries to pin down the exact wording and location of the "two solitudes" reference in the work of the German poet Rainer-Maria Rilke (1875–1926). In July he dispatches two copies of a 582-page typescript of _Two Solitudes_ , one to Blanche Gregory his literary agent in New York and the other to the publisher Duell, Sloan and Pearce. A third copy will go to Collins in Toronto who will publish in Canada.

1945 _Two Solitudes_ is published (publication date is mid-January although copies had been made available in December of 1944). The book is well-received with congratulatory letters from friends and literary associates and excellent reviews with sales reflecting that. The success of _Two Solitudes_ and its financial returns enable MacLennan to resign from Lower Canada College, a situation that he had never enjoyed. He also wins the Governor General's Award for fiction that year.

1948 MacLennan publishes _The Precipice_ , which, while it wins the Governor General's Award for fiction, is not a commercial or critical success.

1949 _Cross Country_ is published, a collection of ten previously published essays and articles, half having appeared in _Maclean's_ magazine. MacLennan wins the Governor General's Award for non-fiction.

1951 With his wife's health beginning to fail and medical bills becoming a burden (there had even been a suggestion the previous year that he accept financial help from the Canadian Writers' Foundation) MacLennan accepts a part-time position in the English Department of McGill University thereby entering the academic profession in which he will remain until his retirement. _Each Man's Son_ is finally published in May in spite of delays. MacLennan teaches two courses at McGill, one on Canadian fiction and the other on English prose. He is in touch with contemporary writing and admires the work of W.O. Mitchell, whose _Who Has Seen the Wind_ is on his assigned reading list. Similarly Evelyn Waugh's _Brideshead Revisited_ is recommended to his English prose class.

1954 _Thirty and Three_ , MacLennan's second collection of essays, is published, winning for him the Governor General's Award for non-fiction.

1957 Dorothy Duncan, MacLennan's wife for twenty-one years, dies after a prolonged struggle with chronic illness.

1959 MacLennan marries Frances Aline Walker, known as Tota, who had been a family friend for some years. _The Watch That Ends the Night_ is published and wins MacLennan's fifth and last Governor General's Award.

1960 _Scotchman's Return and Other Essays_ is published.

1964 MacLennan takes a sabbatical leave from the university and goes to live in Grenoble, France, where he continues writing his next novel and works hard at mastering spoken French.

1966 The Molson Prize, a rich cash award in the second year of its existence, names MacLennan as the recipient of this honour.

1967 _Return of the Sphinx_ is published; MacLennan is inducted as a companion of the Order of Canada.

1968–1969 Alarmed at the general malaise in society and the militancy of the young – student riots in Paris, a mob marching to demand a "McGill français," the burning of the computer centre at Sir George Williams University in Montreal – MacLennan retreats into himself to incubate the themes of personal dysfunction and social alienation that become strong and prophetic elements in his last novel, _Voices in Time_.

1980 _Voices in Time_ is published with publicity releases claiming that it is MacLennan's finest novel. It will also be his last.

1990 MacLennan dies on 9 November at his country home in North Hatley in the Eastern Townships of Quebec.

Note: MacLennan won many awards and distinctions in his time. He was made a chevalier of the National Order of Quebec, was elected a fellow of the Royal Society of Canada, and had honorary degrees conferred on him by several Canadian universities.

A paperback edition published by Stoddart Publishing of Toronto in 1993 in its New Press Canadian Classics Series. The cover image is the painting _Miners' Houses, Glace Bay_ (1925) by Lawren Harris, now in the collection of the Art Gallery of Ontario.
