INTRODUCTION

BEGINNINGS

It is difficult today to understand the extent to which, in the years before the Second World War, Canadian literature was not part of the consciousness of most Canadians. In 1940, an author might have had a fine manuscript, but there was little or no likelihood that the work would make it to the bookstore under the imprint of a Canadian publisher. For a writer like Hugh MacLennan, who had just such a manuscript, the odds against acceptance must have seemed overwhelming. As it turned out, his attempts to find a Canadian publisher were futile, and so an American publisher began MacLennan's career, something he would always remember. At a conference in the late 1980s at Dalhousie University, in the process of delivering the keynote address, he draped his octogenarian frame over a lectern and spoke of his early years as a writer, of the difficulties involved in doing what he had done as a Canadian novelist. He returned again and again to the ways in which Canada had failed to support its own writers until he had dragged it to an awareness of the possibility of a Canadian literature. The irony was never lost upon him that a Canadian writing about Canada should have to go to the United States to be published. Yet this dependence on a foreign market did not deter him from his chosen subject and although he returned to American publishers, it was always as a Canadian writer writing about Canada. Thus his fourth novel, _Each Man's Son_ , set mainly on Cape Breton Island on Canada's east coast, differed from the others only in that it brought him back, perhaps more intensely than in any other of his novels, to his family's roots.

Cape Breton Island had a particular place in the history of the MacLennan family. Here MacLennan's father began his career in 1895, hired by the coal company to treat the many mining injuries, and here Hugh was born in 1907. It was the world of Hugh's earliest years, the world of his father and grandfather. It conserved the Presbyterian world and religious temperament that his ancestors had taken with them from Scotland, which had so strongly influenced his father's and his temperament and would become the thematic centre of his Cape Breton novel. In the case of Cape Breton, MacLennan saw a culture preoccupied not only with the religious but with the romantic heritage of Highland Scotland, although, given the novel's time frame, he was also examining a cultural shift that was to be enacted throughout much of the world in the twentieth century. During this century, almost every people and culture was detached from its ancient days as an agrarian society. In 1913, Cape Breton was forced into the modern world by the development of the steel and coal industry. When they came to Cape Breton Island in the early and mid-nineteenth century, the Scots were drawn by the prospect of land of their own. Among this great wave of immigrants was Neil MacLennan, who in 1832 sailed from Applecross, a community in the shire of Ross and Cromarty, although his roots were in Kintail.1 He left behind the impoverished and limiting world of his forefathers in order to benefit from the land offers of the New World. Perhaps he also sought to leave behind, at least physically, the _Druim nan deur_ , the MacLennan family battle cry, the "ridge of tears." When the Scottish settlers approached the coast of Cape Breton Island they saw before them a forested land that duplicated in miniature the topography of their former home, with the hills shrouded in mist above the peaty valleys, each with its meagre allotment of friable soil. Names redolent of their former towns and villages sprang up all over the island: Inverness, Strathlorne, Dundee, Barra, Iona, Oban, and Skye; even its hills, like John's Hill, acquired a Gaelic name, Ben Eoin. Like Scotland, Cape Breton possessed great beauty, a dangerous beauty Hugh MacLennan would write, a beauty that continued to hold the love of its sons even after they left.

The life of these new communities was the traditional pastoral and agrarian life still dominant in much of the Western world. Their life rhythms were the rhythms of nature, their most important affairs those of family and community. The language they spoke was Gaelic, which, with their Presbyterianism, occupied a prominent position in the foreground of their lives. Their doctrinal disputes were legendary, and in one episode, the body of a woman was shuffled from one community to another in an attempt to find a pastor who shared her particular shading of Presbyterianism. But they also gathered for ceilidhs, great house parties where people talked out their memories, repeating the stories of the community; listened to music; sang endless songs, often melancholy; danced to the fiddle; ate vast amounts; and generally enjoyed themselves. They carried with them the legends of another age, tales of Celtic heroes sometimes told by itinerant bards. This agrarian society was the world of Neil and his son, Daniel. What Neil MacLennan did for the eight years after his arrival in 1832 is not clear, but Nova Scotia land records offer evidence of the presence of the MacLennan family in 1840 at Malagawash, a small community on the southwestern shore of the Bras d'Or Lakes. His land, abutting an area called North Mountain, is not much more favoured by nature than that near Kintail. The next glimpse of the family comes some forty years later when we find a copy of an advertisement in a Sydney newspaper dated 1880 announcing the services of one Duncan McLennan ( _sic_ ), tailor and clothier, whose establishment was located on South Charlotte St., Sydney, where he offered a large stock of "Scotch and Canadian Tweeds and English Coatings," and then in small print, "Which I am prepared to make up in the usual styles at REDUCED PRICES." There seem to be no family legends as to why this generation made the hundred-kilometre move to Sydney, although the town had decent schools and churches. In the next generation the progression from crofter to landowner to merchant and, finally, to professional man was to be completed when Sam, Hugh MacLennan's father and Duncan's son, became a doctor.

Sydney would not long continue as a simple regional centre. From the early 1890s onward, the region was the focus of Canadian and American industrialists who in 1893 founded the Dominion Coal Company with eight collieries as an asset base. In time, the collieries seemed to invade the area, forming a great arc from Glace Bay to New Waterford and on to Sydney Mines, seventy or thereabouts at one time or another in Glace Bay alone. There were many more "bootleg" mines, illegal mines that were holes in the ground, really, and very dangerous, to which the desperate turned in times of need. Each legitimate mine was surmounted by a bankhead and each bankhead was surrounded by the company housing, with everything covered by the eternal coal dust. The labour for this newly built industrial enterprise came first from the rural areas of the island, which had already begun to bleed their population to New England and central Canada. In large numbers they moved from their farms to the new jobs in steel and coal, where they attempted to maintain their pre-industrial culture. These were the men Dr. Sam sewed up and helped save, as well as the casualties of the fights on Saturday night by Senator's Corner, in the centre of Glace Bay. This kaleidoscopic milieu is the world of the novel, in fact not one world but two, the first the dying agrarian culture of olden days, and the second a destructive industrial giant, settling in comfortably to wreak its havoc.

In 1895, when Dr. Sam came to work for a coal company, the burgeoning coal industry offered opportunities to doctors who might have had the usual problems developing a practice and who were not unwilling to deal with a rough clientele. He chose, however, to live some distance from the mines and the centre of the town, and certainly away from company housing, obvious sources of contagion. One anecdote still repeated today by people from his old neighbourhood tells of Dr. Sam returning home after a visit to an infected house, stripping himself in the garage, and then dousing himself with a chemical cleaner with antiseptic properties so that his house would not be contaminated. The Glace Bay town records of 1909 list scarlet fever, diphtheria, smallpox, typhoid fever, and tuberculosis as diseases present in the town. His house, on higher land and partly surrounded by a brook, offered a refuge for his wife and children. It was from here, Elspeth Cameron tells us in her biography of MacLennan, that Hugh ran away at the age of three to become a miner.

How Dr. Sam might have been tried by his circumstances during his years working for the mining company we can never know, but he eventually decided to work toward a specialty, establishing himself and his family in Halifax in 1909. MacLennan would say in an interview in 1973, "I grew up in a mining town,"2 but it was a polite fiction. After the age of five or thereabouts he saw little of Glace Bay, except for a period in early 1918 when, after the Halifax Explosion, he returned to finish his school year. There are no references to this unscheduled return except in a very late interview with fellow Glace Bay native and CBC radio personality Bill McNeil when Hugh was eighty-one: "[W]e went back to Glace Bay for another while, when I went to school there. So all in all I remember the place very well. It was like a frontier town in those years with people coming in from all over the world. I'd stroll over to the main part of town on a Saturday night, where there'd be a lot of those miners with coal dust still on their faces. It seemed there'd always be a fight between two or three of them on Commercial Street at Senator's Corner, with great crowds cheering them on... Oh, she was a wild old town back then."3 The world of the novel, however, makes clear that in transforming Glace Bay into Broughton MacLennan did not leave behind either Cape Breton or his Scottish heritage.

THE MAKING OF A NOVEL

_It is the story of a middle-aged man in search of a son, whom he would equate with a God, a faith – and a purpose in life_.  
Hugh MacLennan

MacLennan's return to familiar ground in his fourth novel reflected, in part, the relatively cool reception of his earlier novel _The Precipice_ (1949) and the critical complaints about his lack of success in portraying an American scene. However, he was intent upon avoiding a "provincial" approach, and in a letter to John Gray, a confidant and his editor at his Canadian publisher, Macmillan, he makes clear his overview of the novel:

It is the first novel I have written with a theme absolutely universal, in the sense that it could exist anywhere on earth and at any time on earth. The whole action occurs in Cape Breton, and though the date is the year before the first World War, my reason for setting the date then is merely because it liberated the theme from niggling details. The date is mentioned only once in the whole book and I believe the reader is quite unconscious of it. The speech, the colour – all of that is Cape Breton. But these things are secondary. The book moves almost as fast as does _Barometer Rising_ , but it certainly has a great deal more depth and works itself out of terms of character. As I said to Little, Brown [his American publisher] (because the chief character is a doctor) it is a book not about a doctor, but about a man who happens to be a doctor, a book not about Canada, but by a man who happens to be a Canadian. And of course, it is about a good deal more than a single man.4

Earlier, he had been concerned that Canadian novelists might need to make the reader aware of the history and the geography of this new breed of novel. In 1952, just after publishing _Each Man's Son_ but referring to _Barometer Rising_ , he wrote that "it seemed to me that for some years to come the Canadian novelist would have to pay a great deal of attention to the background in which he set his stories. He must describe, and if necessary, define the social values which dominate the Canadian scene, and do so in such a way as to make them appear interesting and important to foreigners. Whether he liked it or not, he must for a time be something of a geographer, an historian and a sociologist."5 But in this novel as in his earlier novels (and as he notes in an earlier letter dated 1941), his focus was on "the artist's ability to achieve a universality out of a native background"6 and he would use that insight in his new novel. He sought, again to quote Elspeth Cameron, a story "as primitive as anything in Greek tragedy."7 The reference to Greek tragedy suggests both the relentlessness of the action of the drama and the inevitability of its deadly consequence. It suggests also the intervention of the gods (or one God anyway) and the presence of the ancient energies for good and evil. It evokes a toughness of temperament in the face of battle and a refusal to consider the niceties of ordinary life, not merely the absence of courtesy (for that can be present on the battlefield) but the refusal to bend beliefs that results in a certain brutality coexisting with the tender emotions essential to human existence. Such a primitive nature comes presumably from the absence of civilizing influences. This conclusion about the influence of Greek tragedy is curious, given that his father had argued that the classics brought us civilization. Perhaps "primitive" Cape Breton had little of the intellectual complexity of modern life and so in the simplicity of its beliefs it spoke of that other, more ancient world.

Elspeth Cameron suggests that the state of MacLennan's wife's health and the resulting expenses not only hastened the completion of _Each Man's Son_ but made him passive in the face of pressures for change from his new American publisher, the Boston firm Little, Brown. The notorious "Author's Note," which he opposed vehemently, was a publisher's imposition meant to provide background for the American reader. Edmund Wilson's gloss on the note is appropriately caustic: "It is curious, but characteristic of our assumption of the remoteness of Canada, that any such explanation should have been thought to be necessary – and, of all things, an explanation of Calvinism in of all places, Boston."8 Wilson adds that MacLennan later wrote to him to make the point that the note "was not written to attack or even to emphasize Calvinism." But the note guaranteed that this particular aspect of the book would almost certainly trump others and that the Scottish "adventure" of the last four hundred years, with its strong romantic nature derived from the Celtic culture, would be secondary. Yet it is hard to avoid an intense focus on the Calvinist protagonist, Dr. Ainslie, particularly since this theme was a living reality in MacLennan's own life, something he felt forced to confront: "My moral quarrel with Calvinism was not that it denied realities, but that it inculcated into children the idea that God was each man's personal enemy, and that a man committed a sin merely by existing."9 Dr. Ainslie is the child whose understanding was so terribly distorted, and the individual who is destined to overthrow Calvinism. As MacLennan wrote, after having a dream about Calvin, "one of these days I'll settle that bastard somehow or other."10

The second area of contention with his editors was the conclusion. In the first submitted manuscript Archie MacNeil is alive and in jail and the book ends with a court scene. To please his editor MacLennan killed Archie and ended the novel shortly after the discovery of the double murder. But his friend Gray, who was part of the discussion, suggested further changes: "Alan should be older than five; many of the scenes concerning Mollie should be removed; the Cape Breton stories should be made more organic; and the period of the story should be defined more clearly."11 Although no doubt outraged, MacLennan was still able to thank those who proffered suggestions: "You, in your own criticisms of _Each Man's Son_ , made me feel that you were sitting right inside my mind, perceiving my intentions better than I could perceive them myself."12 However, Gray went farther, showing the manuscript to an English graduate student, L.A.A. Harding, who delivered a rather extensive critique: "I think it misses being a very good novel for various reasons," he wrote, complaining about the female characterization, the sense that we are reading reportage and not a novel, and his feeling that the final chapter "disrupts the unity of the book." Grey was so incautious as to allow MacLennan to see a copy, with what effect we can judge by the reply. A sentence will suffice to show his anger. "The Ph.D.'s report was clever, intelligent and not much more irrelevant than such academic judgments usually are; the bulk of the criticism developed from the man's own premises, which managed to blind him to the nature of the book I have written."13 MacLennan was suggesting, in effect, a hierarchy in which the academic critic was located well below the writer. He did cut the final chapter but touched little else. The book, published on 2 April 1951, was only a hair away from being marketed under the slogan "The first real Calvinistic novel in years." What the effect on its reception might have been we can only imagine. The novel did well but not as well as he had expected. The reviewers were reasonably accommodating, finding the "usual" MacLennan themes, but were most appreciative of the advance the novel represented in formal terms. It was his best novel yet and confirmed his choice of a Canadian subject. Its sales, however, were insufficient to maintain him as a writer and, in the spring of 1951, he applied for and got a part-time job at McGill University. Thus the novel marked a kind of watershed in his life and career.

MACLENNAN CRITICISM: AN OVERVIEW

Interestingly, newspaper and periodical reviews of Hugh MacLennan's novels from _Barometer Rising_ (1941) onwards were positive, sometimes extravagantly so, in welcoming his work as the first by a "Canadian" novelist, that is, a novelist who sought to make Canada a vibrant part of the novel. The extent to which this preoccupation with major Canadian historical themes was important to reviewers became clear in the negative critical response to his third volume, _The Precipice_ (1948), which reviewers saw as too American. His fourth novel, _Each Man's Son_ , was much more kindly received, at least partly because of its Canadian setting. Academic recognition of MacLennan's work was longer in coming, and extensive examination of MacLennan's work really began only in the late 1960s with the rise of Canadian literature as a discipline. We should remember that at that point Canadian universities were still trying to find a place for Canadian literature. I vividly remember a challenge a female student offered the professor, who was also head of the English department, in one of my undergraduate classes in 1966: "Why don't we have courses in Canadian literature?" she asked. He replied, "What literature?" – a none-too-subtle restatement of Gertrude Stein's famous remark about her hometown of Oakland, "There is no there there."

Some of the earliest academic comments on MacLennan's oeuvre come from Hugo McPherson, who, in "The Novels of Hugh MacLennan" (1951), points to MacLennan's formal problems: "The difficulty of having symbolic structures and fully individuated characters... The increasingly complex nature of MacLennan's artistic vision has not been fully supported by parallel developments in technique" (24). In a more positive but nationalist light, Maritime critic Desmond Pacey, in _Creative Writing in Canada_ (1961), refers to MacLennan's didacticism but sees his work as "the most direct reflection in fiction of the growing national self-consciousness which has been such a conspicuous feature of recent Canadian history" (217). A few years later Edmund Wilson, an American critic of no mean stature, although with a skewed vision of the developments in Canadian literature because of his distance from the scene, was quick to pay homage in _O Canada_ (1964) to "a Canadian way of looking at things," seeing in the novel a distinctive Canadian voice, although he felt it was old-fashioned. References such as these to nationalism, didacticism, and insecurity in formal matters had been mentioned in early book reviews and would be actively discussed in MacLennan criticism for many years to come.

The late 1960s and early 1970s saw a considerable rise in academic interest in MacLennan, with five monographs appearing within a five-year period and three in 1969 alone. Their reception allows us to look at academic consideration of MacLennan, his novels, and their place in Canadian literature. The early focus inevitably dealt first with MacLennan's Canadianness, his willingness to deal with the Canadian landscape and the Canadian character, followed by his preoccupation with puritanism and, conversely, with the materialism of the modern age. He was, many contended, a novelist of ideas. Inevitably, they concluded that both characterization and structural principles suffered as a result. Generally, they gave him his due as a pioneer, but always with a sense that any writer who is the earliest in a field may not be able to master all of the elements involved in a novel's construction.

In his monograph _Hugh MacLennan_ (1969), Peter Buitenhuis, a colleague of MacLennan at McGill University (and therefore perhaps a little too close for forthright criticism), points to MacLennan's didacticism as well as his perception of the movement toward decolonization. He traces MacLennan's need to make things happen in Canada in fiction and by doing so to define the territory and people of Canada. Inevitably, he also refers to MacLennan's struggle with Presbyterianism and puritanism. George Woodcock's _Hugh MacLennan_ (1969) stresses not only MacLennan's didacticism and nationalism but also his concern with spiritual beliefs and his repudiation of Calvinism, as well as his essential conservatism in formal terms. He emphasizes MacLennan as a social novelist, which perhaps reveals Woodcock's own social and political interests. The clarity with which he speaks of MacLennan's relationship with Canadian history and geography makes his description still worth reading. In _Northern Spring_ (1987) he covers many of the same ideas, noting again the tendency to the didactic (83), although he does say that _Each Man's Son_ ( _EMS_ ) is not marred by it. He is most interested in the ways MacLennan uses classical texts, particularly the Odyssean imagery, and sees the "Odyssean pattern of the wanderer returning to the waiting woman" (89) as a dominating pattern in the novels. Robert Cockburn, in _The Novels of Hugh MacLennan_ (1969), is perhaps the most severe of his early critics, finding MacLennan to be "an ambitious and interesting but profoundly flawed novelist" (8). He sees those flaws as arising from his use of non-fiction techniques in his fiction (a point that postmodern critics would see in an entirely different light). He also suggests that MacLennan's romanticism did not help him create credible worlds. Alec Lucas, in _Hugh MacLennan_ (1970), approaches the novels from a different vantage point. He investigates the formal elements of the novels and calls attention to the ways in which MacLennan has, for example, used aspects of popular romance in his work (in his examination of marriage and the inevitable triangle of which we have three variants in the novel, or the device of the husband who suddenly returns). He also calls our attention to the stiffness of some of the characters, fathers in particular. MacLennan's great problem, suggests Lucas, is his inability to fuse the idea and the narrative to create a situation that works on both levels. In her _The Immoral Moralists: Hugh MacLennan and Leonard Cohen_ (1972), Patricia Morley questions MacLennan's portrayal of puritanism as a wholly negative force.

Paul Goetsch, a German critic whose book (in German) on MacLennan was the earliest work on the author, writes in 1973 in an introduction to a later work, _Critical Views on Canadian Writers: Hugh MacLennan_ (1973), that it is "not unfair to say that, judged in international terms, he is at best a good minor postmodernist writer though he is a novelist of major importance for Canada and the history of Canadian fiction." The pressure to place an important national novelist in a larger context is always salutary. T.D. MacLulich's criticisms were made in _Hugh MacLennan_ (1983), number 708 in the American publisher Twayne's World Authors series. His overview of MacLennan calls particular attention to the way in which he "depicts characters who resist the inertia and restrictiveness of a philistine society and who labor under the inhibitions left by a puritanical family background" (1). These Calvinist elements are the focus of his interest in _EMS_. His overall assessment of MacLennan's achievement is "uneven," though he thinks there are impressive elements in the mix. Three interviews by Ronald Sutherland (1970), Donald Cameron (1973), and Alan Twigg (1981) from this period should also be read. As MacLennan was obviously near the end of his writing career, they have a certain valedictory quality but give us a useful gloss on MacLennan's interests throughout his career.

Approximately a decade after the first burst of attention, Elspeth Cameron's two works appeared, _Hugh MacLennan: A Writer's Life_ (1981) and _Hugh MacLennan: 1982 Proceedings of the MacLennan Conference at University College_ (1982). Cameron's biography quickly established itself as the one indispensable tool for the study of MacLennan and his work. She is careful with biographical material, having talked extensively with her subject, and the only complaint might be that she sometimes seems reluctant to probe too deeply. She deals most convincingly with MacLennan the writer, and her early access to a vast amount of unpublished material allows us to understand MacLennan the novelist as he laboured to create his novels. Helen Hoy's survey of criticism of MacLennan in _Hugh MacLennan and His Works_ emphasizes the need for more formal criticism that would take us past the earlier academic judgment. Her implicit suggestion that there is more to investigate and discover about MacLennan's work is salutary and still timely.

Discussions of MacLennan's work have been less frequent since the mid-1980s. The world of literature is just as susceptible to fashion as any other facet of human activity. Nationalism, for example, must seem to the young to be a battle that has already been fought, and MacLennan's concerns with materialism or spirituality have been dealt with in many more recent works by other Canadian authors. He does retain a certain currency in regional studies, as we see in Janice Kulyk Keefer's _Under Eastern Eyes_ (1987), where MacLennan is taken to task for his abandonment of "any real commitment to the Maritimes" (138) as well as his avoidance of the discussion of socio-political issues. She finds the way he moves from a meaningful consideration of social forces to focus on "individual ambition, endurance, and skill" unfortunate (141). In the 1990s Barbara Pell, in _Faith and Fiction: A Theological Critique of the Narrative Strategies of Hugh MacLennan and Morley Callaghan_ (1998), examines the roots of MacLennan's spiritual beliefs and picks up the work of critics like Morley and Davis (see below). She uncovers the logic behind MacLennan's need to rid himself of Calvinism and the paths he took to do so, ending by describing how his new concept of God parallels that found in some strands of the modern theology of Paul Tillich, who emphasizes the need for a re-conceptualization of the Godhead, beginning with a rejection of the old, a modern example of the sort of action anticipated in Nietzsche's famous statement "God is dead."

CRITICISM OF _EACH MAN'S SON_

Specific considerations of _Each Man's Son_ include many of the elements of praise and concern mentioned above. Contemporary reviews of _EMS_ welcomed MacLennan's return to Canadian topics and a Canadian setting and were quick to note the increased formal mastery demonstrated in the novel, sometimes described as taut and well-constructed. Most of the complaints were aimed at the ending and the didactic tone, which sometimes seemed to make the ideas more important than the people. The "Author's Note" was often seen as a mistake by Canadian critics, although not by American critics, for telling too much and diffusing the climax. Reaction, however, was generally positive. George Woodcock points to the daring nature of the novel, with its subject the author's father's generation, the most difficult of all time periods for any author to understand.14 But he also points to the classical influence as well as the economic critique that is part of the novel. Joyce Marshall, a novelist herself, sees strengths in the handling of landscape, the "evocative descriptive material about Cape Breton," but sees MacLennan as "too persistent in his comments and explanations." She finds the characterization so weak that "ultimately, Calvinism must remain the true hero of the book." Presumably she means that Calvinism is the focus of our attention rather than Dr. Ainslie who struggles with it – it is difficult to think of Calvinism as a hero.

In early academic comment on the novel, Hugo McPherson sees MacLennan's problems as primarily formal. How, for instance, do the three deaths at the end of _EMS_ relate to the Calvinism of the novel? He damns with lavish praise when he notes that MacLennan's ideas command "our respect and admiration."15 A few years later Desmond Pacey (1961) simplistically (and somewhat wrongheadedly) describes the novel as "a regional study of a Cape Breton coal-mining town in the years immediately preceding World War I" and a chronicle of "the lives of simple people in an isolated settlement."16 Glace Bay (i.e. Broughton) was most certainly not isolated in any sense of the word. Edmund Wilson also refers to the novel, calling attention to the insecurity of its structure, and suggesting curiously that the novel "has been willed rather than lived"17 and that in the ending MacLennan had surrendered to "a Scotchman's melodramatic sentiment." He had derived from his Presbyterianism, said Wilson, "the compulsive passages of preaching which occasionally interrupt his narrative."18 However, Wilson finds that "within its more restricted limits, it is a much more satisfying novel than _The Precipice_."19

Criticism at the end of the 1960s treated _EMS_ rather more positively than MacLennan's two previous novels. Robert Cockburn is uncharacteristically complimentary toward the novel, judging it to be MacLennan's best. He sees the four characters around which the novel is built as "brilliantly portrayed," although he is highly provocative in his conclusion: "Did he master the art of fiction in _Each Man's Son?_ Almost."20 Alec Lucas does not offer an extended discussion of the novel in his 1969 monograph, but in an introduction to the 1961 reprinting of the novel in paperback he gives a tightly argued interpretation. His focus here is on the religious argument of the novel, highlighting the extent to which MacLennan's morbid fascination with Calvinism dominates. He is particularly adept at teasing apart the elements of the doctor's plot to displace Mollie as Alan's parent. He then shows how the doctor's growing understanding of his behaviour provokes the spiritual crisis that allows him finally to throw off Calvinist teachings and to embrace the idea that "God is love."21 "Only by giving in love, not through restraint, can man break the barriers between self and society and self and God."22 A discerning reader might, however, ask whether it is clear by the end of the novel that the doctor is interested in God. On the whole, Lucas provides careful analysis rather than judgments. Peter Buitenhuis is as positive as Cockburn in his consideration of the novel: "In _Each Man's Son_ , MacLennan goes far deeper into the motivations and psychological structures of his characters, particularly with Dr. Ainslie, and thus writes a more humane book than he had done before" (50). He compares the novel favourably to Hawthorne's work exploring puritanism. He is also impressed by the "attention to details of landscape."23 Buitenhuis adds that EMS "demonstrated a sureness of touch that gives the novel a new note of authority."24 Woodcock, in his discussion of the novel in his 1969 monograph, entitles the chapter "Homer and Calvin," giving us fair warning of his primary concerns. He sees _EMS_ as "a threnody on the old Highland culture" calling our attention to MacLennan's linking of Highland Scotland and ancient Greece. Woodcock is more specific in finding connections to Homer's _Odyssey_ , for "in _Each Man's Son_ MacLennan returns emphatically – even if unconsciously – to the counter-pointed pattern of the Odyssey."25 He treats "the theme of Calvinist guilt" in a less determined fashion but does write of "a little society bound together by a common faith in its own damnation."26 In _Northern Spring_ (1987), Woodcock returns to _EMS_ but finds it less Odyssean and more Oedipal, a tale of fathers and sons. Patricia Morley focuses on the Calvinism, discovering that MacLennan has unconsciously pointed out some of the ways in which the Calvinism he so detests has something positive to offer. John Moss, in his _Reader's Guide to the Canadian Novel_ (1972), a judgment meant for the general public rather than a university audience, calls _EMS_ "the least pretentious of his novels... well-balanced, subtle... graphic, dramatic, highly moving" (179–80). He is grandly complimentary in finding the novel "the work that most surely guarantees his place at the very centre of the Canadian literary pantheon." A more distanced view, presbyopic in fact, is that of Hermann Boeschenstein, who calls _EMS_ "a regional novel about the Gaelic world of Cape Breton with its characteristic serenity and attendant hardships."27

Several articles devoted to _EMS_ are worth our attention. Laurel Boone's " _Each Man's Son_ : Romance in Disguise" (1979) takes up part of Lucas's argument from his 1969 monograph in looking at the formal elements of the novel. She contends that MacLennan's expressed need to appeal to as large an audience as possible results in an "emphasis on action and public appeal" (147), characteristics best expressed in the romance form. She goes on to show how the formal elements of the novel are consistent with that form and argues that MacLennan has absorbed these elements to good effect. In her view, _EMS_ "is, structurally at least, MacLennan's best novel, for he has restrained his didactic urge," presumably because the romance does not allow for that sort of comment. However, "the tension between romance and realism finally fractures the book" (155–6). David J. Dooley's " _Each Man's Son_ : The Daemon of Hope and Imagination" (1980) focuses on Dr. Ainslie. Dooley examines the expectations within Presbyterianism that cause the doctor to act the way he does in the "Calvinistic emphasis on self-discipline and repression of feeling" (71). However, when Ainslie decides to pursue guardianship of the boy he violates his own belief in the autonomy of the individual. His crisis comes to a head when he realizes what he has done and intends to do; only then is it possible for him to see that "no," the rejection of the past, is not enough. There must be something positive to accede to and that, he discovers, is love, through which "the ancient curse has been overcome" (74). Marilyn J. Davis's article "Fathers and Sons" also examines the impact of Presbyterianism on the doctor. Her differentiation of Calvinism and Puritanism (usually used interchangeably) is very useful and clarifies Dr. Ainslie's struggles. She shows how the guilt that is his father's gift to him acts as a filter that makes it impossible for him to see the world around him except as contaminated by the depravity natural to the human condition. In order to find out what is truly valuable in his life he must renounce his guilt, which he finally does in his embrace of love. His emotional responses are then no longer his enemies. He must, as Sylvia Plath could have told him, "kill" his father if he is to be a worthy father himself.

After Elspeth Cameron's major works, the 1980s were relatively sparse in critical comment. Janice Kulyk Keefer's reading of MacLennan's world through the lens of social theory in _Under Eastern Eyes_ (1987) reveals the extent to which MacLennan's Cape Breton is a personal creation. She also sees a refusal to deal with the social issues of the day in the real Cape Breton. In _Hugh MacLennan and His Works_ , Helen Hoy (1990) offers both a masterful survey of critical comment up to that point and also a persuasive reading of the novel. She finds the structure of _EMS_ of particular interest and shows how a series of parallels perform a dance in counterpoint that speeds the novel to its climax. She also points out the difficulties with respect to point of view. A first-person narrative would have placed the doctor and his religious and intellectual struggles much more securely at the centre of the novel.

In the 1990s, Barbara Pell builds on earlier discussion of the religious elements in the book in _Faith and Fiction_ (1998), pointing to MacLennan's probing of "the existential meaninglessness that accompanies a renunciation of religion" (38). She calls attention to the sort of religious attitude represented by Mrs. MacCuish, an embittered old woman who most clearly projects MacLennan's characterization of Calvinist heritage as a curse with a perverse insistence that it be inflicted upon the young. Once the young are inculcated, only the most determined can escape. It is for this reason that Dr. Ainslie's rejection of his religion and his embrace of science in no way lessen the influence of Calvinism in his life. Keeping Pell's comments in mind, it seems improbable that Ainslie would get over his problem in one flash of insight, no matter how profound – something that MacLennan probably recognized, as suggested by his tentative statement in Donald Cameron's interview in the early 1970s: "I think I'm at last over Calvinism."28 MacLennan was in his mid-sixties at the time. Only Dr. Ainslie's realization of love, which comes at the very end of the novel, can truly save him. Pell believes that in _EMS_ MacLennan "achieved his best integration of religious vision and realistic form up to this point" (43) but she sees the ending as melodramatic. She also praises his characterization (except for Camire) of rounded individuals, although she concludes that overall, "his strength lies in his vision and his weakness in his art," a conclusion that can seem to welcome what others have seen as a major flaw, his didacticism.

Perhaps we should end an overview of comments about the novel with those of Alistair MacLeod who, just after MacLennan's death, tried, as a writer and fellow Cape Bretoner, to give his own perspective on MacLennan and his work. He is particularly taken – perhaps "disappointed" would be a better word – by MacLennan's dismissal of his Highland heritage in an embrace of the AngloSaxon, which MacLeod sees as an expression of MacLennan's acceptance of his father's pronounced leaning to modernism. But he also detects an ambivalence in MacLennan's attitudes: the Highlanders often steal the show, striking us as more human because they continue to be attached to the old life of Cape Breton. The essay offers a loving gloss on the works of an influential Canadian writer.

_EACH MAN'S SON_ : A READING

No age is free of tensions arising from major social and cultural change, but some ages and some peoples must cope with more than their fair share. As the well-known curse goes, "May you live in interesting times." This was the fate of Cape Breton Island and its people in the years before the First World War.

Our introduction to the Cape Breton of _Each Man's Son_ and its inhabitants takes place on one of the island's shores. A woman is watching a child building an elaborate sand castle. Around them we see the sand, the ocean waves, the moon, and in the distance, a sailing vessel that follows the wind. So idyllic does it seem that we might imagine ourselves in another Eden or, at the least, the healing world of nature. But we are not allowed to harbour this illusion for long. A shrill industrial whistle destroys the idyll. The woman and boy, Mollie MacNeil and her son, Alan, move by degrees from the shore inland until they find themselves amid the uniformity of a company town. This is Broughton, its rows of identical houses proclaiming its industrial identity, while the great piles of coal, "black and monstrous" next to a bankhead, the mine's entrance, make clear the reason for its existence. The piles and the bankhead dominate the landscape. In front of the houses we see the miners bending over washtubs as they or their wives try to scrub away the coal stains from their bodies. It will be hard to lose sight of the mines, which are so potent a force in these people's lives, although MacLennan shows the reader nothing of the actual mining, not the miners confronting the demanding work at the coal face, nor the camaraderie and humour found there amid the ever-present dangers, nor the selfless actions in times of disaster. Thus it is a dark scene that confronts the reader in this introduction to a typical Broughton street.

The scene's darkness is both a recognition of industrial exploitation in the modern world and a representation of the fallen world so vividly present in Calvinism, the other constant in MacLennan's Cape Breton, with its belief in the fallen nature of the earth and its inhabitants. The miners, like men under the stain of original sin, try in vain to clean away the grime of the coal dust, a dust that promises them the dismally unheroic death of black lung disease. They are effectively trapped in the mines if for no other reason than their debts to the company store. They have had little choice in their lives: Highland warriors in ancient days and then farmers. Traditional and agrarian activities in Cape Breton have been insufficient to support them so they have been forced to move into the industrial world, bringing with them a set of values strongly opposed to the industrial value system in which they now must live. The members of this community have been characterized by some critics as "child-like primitives," but the judgment is hasty as well as condescending. MacLennan, whether he was aware of it or not, has allowed them an intriguing complexity.

Having lived a traditional life in Cape Breton's rural areas – traditional because the rhythms of nature were overlaid by an ancient Celtic culture – they are now involved in an enterprise that requires only physical strength in a succession of twelve-hour days. The miners are, like one of the novel's protagonists, the boxer Archie MacNeil, warriors in exile and at a loss as to how to behave in this new industrial world. MacLennan shows them beginning to accept the imperatives of the culture into which they have been thrust and reveals as well, though again perhaps unconsciously, the emotional turbulence that results from such massive change. Inevitably, the hierarchy of values dominant in any new world view condemns the values that preceded it, and so these Cape Breton Scots must purge themselves of the old. But human beings cannot embrace the future in a day. This gives rise to an intermediate state of confusion, a confusion vividly present among the Highlanders we encounter in the novel. The remnants of Gaelic and its oral culture are heard in the singing of Archie and Angus the Barraman, in the lilt of spoken Gaelic that shapes their speech patterns, and in the sense of community as extended family that reveals itself at different times in the action. These elements show us not the simplicity of these folk but rather the extent to which they have been called upon to respond to complex change. They are caught between two worlds, a space occupied just as surely by the novel's two main protagonists, Dr. Dan Ainslie and Archie MacNeil. In this tapestry of change, both Daniel Ainslie and Archie MacNeil work out their fates.

Dr. Ainslie has through hard work become a highly respected surgeon, and Archie MacNeil, a miner turned boxer, has through his talent made himself middleweight champion of Canada. Both men are what the world calls heroes in that, without meaning to, they have a profound and often uplifting impact upon the lives of others through the exercise of their talents. However, their own lives are in incredible disorder. Archie has almost entirely abandoned his family in his quest to become world champion and has placed himself in the hands of men whose intention is to exploit his talents. Dr. Ainslie, as well, has effectively destroyed his family. He has rendered his wife sterile by his own hand, and, by his own admission, he is dissatisfied with his life. The boxer goes about his training for what will be his last fight, not acknowledging the possibility of defeat, while the doctor must battle to decide how and if he can change his life to give it some meaning. But, like the farmers turned miners, neither man can truly cope with the changes that have overtaken or are about to overtake them, and so each man will see his life spiral out of control, the boxer through destruction of his body and brain, and the doctor, paradoxically, through the possibility that joy might once again enter his life.

However, before those moments of joy, the doctor is clearly a tortured soul. He has achieved professional success and the admiration of many but has discovered that this success means nothing because he still unconsciously carries the negative emotional baggage associated with his Presbyterian roots. All the delights of the earth are ashes in his mouth because he feels, just as he has been taught to feel, that these pleasures are contaminated by man's first sin and the almost inevitable prospect of eternal damnation. He has sought to empty himself of these beliefs by embracing science as his new faith, but the transfer has not been successful and his life has resolved into a curious futility. He sees day piled upon day to no discernable purpose. His wife, who should be at the centre of his life, has been pushed away because of the shame he feels when he embraces her and his guilt because he has (for good medical reasons) made her sterile. To make clear the extent of this disaffection, MacLennan offers the reader other examples in the community. Mrs. MacCuish, a malevolent, self-appointed (and mad) prophet, whose exhortations reveal the vast discomfort of a soul devoured by a dogma, and Jimmie MacGillivray, the saloonkeeper, whose stomach pains tell him that he has displeased the Lord and who takes momentary shelter in hypocrisy, reveal the vast pain that grows from Calvinist doctrines. Angus the Barraman, a mineworker and a Catholic (perhaps a Jansenist), also believes his thoughts of pleasure must be hidden in gloom to avert the eye of a God intent on crushing human dreams of happiness.

Dr. Ainslie is completely caught in the web of Calvinism that has both made and unmade him. It has driven him with ruthless clarity to his professional achievements because he once believed that such success might reveal God's favour. Yet as we see in his words and actions, he seems also to feel that to glory in worldly achievements is to consort with everything contaminated by the fallen nature of the world. It is not for nothing that MacLennan in his "Author's Note" calls Calvinism a curse. It matters little that the doctor has rejected these beliefs. As he discovers, dismissal of one set of beliefs followed by intellectual assent to another set is only the first step on a long journey. The old Calvinist imperatives live on as subterranean forces, driving him to keep himself occupied to avoid the issue. He may love his wife but he refuses to enjoy her body because at some level he continues to fear the wrath of the God of the Old Testament who scrutinizes his every breath and who has no doubt already found him wanting. It is not unnatural, therefore, that he should find his emotional life in chaos, or that he should seek an end to this desolation. He is particularly aware that he has lost any sense of purpose in his life. His earlier rejection of religion meant the loss of a transcendent purpose, and now he seems to have also lost the consolation of a sense of purpose in his scientific activities. In his empty universe, there seems no point to life at all, a state made worse because the doctor refuses any aid from those who could and would help him. Until he meets Archie's young son, Alan, he searches in vain for relief from his burden of guilt and depression.

Archie MacNeil, the boxer, carries little of this kind of burden. His purpose in life is clear: to be world champion. He hates the new, industrial world of the mines, and working in the mines would be his fate if he were to remain in Broughton. For four years, he has therefore been living and fighting in the United States. For the moment, and far from home as he trains for his next fight in New Jersey, he pursues pleasures – alcohol mostly, and women – that Dr. Ainslie would subconsciously believe to be signs of depravity and ultimate damnation. Like the warrior he is, he glories in his body and so has neither the doctor's religious scruples nor his doubts. Archie "has gone out into the world" (11), as his son describes it, to seek his fortune. In all such tales, even ones as grand as Homer's _Odyssey_ , there should be a triumphal homecoming after a long and arduous journey. Husband will seek the embrace of his wife, and great happiness will ensue. But Archie fits awkwardly in such a tale. Unlike Odysseus, he is neither clever nor resourceful, having left Broughton with no resource other than his beautiful body and its talent. If we seek a classical parallel, it would be to Ajax, like Odysseus a Greek hero of the Trojan War but, unlike Odysseus, limited in his comprehension of the world. He is another splendid masculine figure in another culture that worshipped the hyper-male physique of the warrior. The comparison is apt but unhappily so, since as some readers will recall Ajax is driven mad and brings about his own death. A descendent of the clan fighters of the Scottish Highlands, Archie has all the qualities needed in battle – physical courage, tolerance of pain, and clear focus in deadly personal encounters – but there is no need of knights in the modern world, no matter how attractive they might be, particularly one so lacking in modern sophistication. Thus, like his ancestors, he is susceptible to the wiles of men "from away," men who have made it their business to work cunningly for their own profit, who will make sure that such romantic nonsense will not survive in the modern world. They have lured Archie to his destruction. But the author is complicit throughout: though he chronicles Archie's decline meticulously and with a good deal of compassion, he permits himself little pity for a man so clearly a throwback to an earlier time. Nor is MacLennan any less ruthless toward the culture itself, the traditional Celtic culture of Cape Breton that has somehow survived into a modern world that clearly has no place for it.

This ruthlessness also informs Dr. Ainslie's initial single-minded rejection of the traditional culture of Cape Breton. He too has embraced the modern world unequivocally. His commitment to medicine is absolute, an acceptance of its imperatives all the more important in that it offered him a new belief structure when he needed one as a substitute for religion. Just as he once turned to science as a substitute, he now seeks a new culture and a new heritage, but his readings of ancient Greek, dictionary in hand, give him little because, though the stories are wonderful, they are not his stories. He also seems blind to the fact that his Homeric texts, like _TheOdyssey_, are filled with images that would not be out of place in earlier days in the Scottish Highlands. Unlike Odysseus, whose adventures he reads nightly, he has little of the exuberance of his Greek hero in his nature, no largeness of spirit, no sense of delight in the pleasures of the flesh, no desperate need for adventure, and no joy in surviving the perils of the world. He is unaware of the extent to which his rejection of his heritage has diminished him. In fact, his desire to leave it behind has contributed to the void in his life. If, unlike Odysseus, he does not seek to be a familiar of the gods, raising only scientific idols such as Cushing, the brain specialist in Boston, he has also ruthlessly stripped himself of cultural icons he might have had. MacLennan has made his own position clear by refusing all reference to cultural figures except in Angus the Barraman's stories of Giant MacAskill, a popular nineteenth-century personality, famous for his height and strength and his association with P.T. Barnum, the American entertainment producer. Dr. Ainslie, having so starkly stripped himself of his heritage and religion, has helped create the void he feels so acutely. And it is no wonder that its contemplation fills him with dread, or that he permits himself behaviour he would not otherwise have countenanced.

The questions that face him are so daunting that he decides to avoid them all by sinking into an orgy of work. He offers splendid treatment to his patients, listening to their fears and hopes because that is what he must do to heal them. How attentive he is to Billy Blackett, the Newfoundland lad who has lost six of his fingers. He reaches out to Billy to convince him that he can still make something of himself by using his brain to create new purpose in his life, and the reader nods approvingly as Billy sets out to follow the doctor's plan. But the doctor cannot manage a similar transformation for himself. What can he do, burdened as he is by the Calvinism of his father, which defines him as unworthy and powerless? He finds an answer in the shape of a child, a possible son. He cannot be objective when he finds a gifted and affectionate child in Archie Mac-Neil's seven-year-old boy, Alan, who has everything he wants in a son. The surge of joy he feels after encountering Alan is completely disorienting, both psychologically and morally. In this overwhelming compulsion to be a father, he quickly marks Alan as his own. When fortune, in the guise of an attack of appendicitis, delivers the boy into his hands, he usurps Mollie's authority and transforms his role as doctor into that of a virtual guardian. The boy is made to come to his house, where he is given food, books, and general care in an attempt to seduce him, despite Mollie's resistance as well as that of Dr. Ainslie's wife, who begins to see what he has become.

The doctor himself is unable to perceive the extent to which he has lost himself. His new anomie-driven amorality transforms him into the patriarchal father because he cannot yet see the extent of his unconscious emulation of his own parent. Nor does he yet see the egregious nature of his father's behaviour, a man who in financing the building of a barn starved his family, leading to the death of Ainslie's mother. He feels free to embark on a dire campaign to attain his goal. His male arrogance leads him to believe that the women in his life are ultimately unimportant and so can be bent to his will, and he follows this path ruthlessly. He savours his extravagant emotions and their delights even as he makes his plans. Dr. Dougald MacKenzie, his Nestor, the wise man of the tale and his surrogate father, accuses him of turning Alan MacNeil into a substitute for his lost God, a point he will later concede. But for now he will do anything to maintain his glorious rediscovery of what he assumes to be love. When he sees Alan among the ruins of Louisburg, he knows that the world has become a different place and he sets out like the warrior he has become to raid a camp in which he has spied a treasure. He fails to see that he has become as bloody-minded as Archie in a fight, and in some ways as violent.

In embracing moral violence, Ainslie invites comparison with Archie, whose métier clearly involves violence of a more basic kind. Yet our first perception is of the strong contrast between the two: Archie's intellectual passivity highlights the doctor's cerebral energies, while a physical comparison points to their disparate endowments. MacLennan's descriptions of Archie's nakedness present us with the glory of the male physique. Mollie remembers with a thrill the masculinity he projected when they married: "His lovely body – wide in the shoulders, narrow in the waist with rippling muscles all over" (19). Of the doctor we see only his "soft black hair... lithe physique" (29), and his large dark eyes. His body is of no interest to him, the fine movements of his hands excepted, because of his emphasis upon the life of the intellect. If he has any attitude toward his body it is one of dislike, because he resembles his dark-haired mother, whom he remembers as weak and unreliable, and not his burly and red-headed father, who in his memory possessed an abundance of physical and moral strength. Yet, as we observe the two, we find that he and Archie are not so different. The brain operation we see the doctor perform is presented in terms similar to those used to describe Archie's skills as a boxer, and the feelings the doctor experiences after a successful operation are like those Archie experiences after an excellent sparring session, a resemblance the author makes explicit when he has Dr. Doucette ask Ainslie whether he feels like having sex after a difficult and successful operation. The doctor's resemblance to Archie extends even to the physical dexterity each demonstrates. Both have a talent that expresses itself through their muscles: the doctor in his hands and fingers as he operates and Archie in the strength and speed of his arms and fists when he knocks his opponent to the canvas. They glory in their skills and each exudes an unapologetic arrogance in their moments of victory. Moreover, it is clear that the doctor has a physical courage equal to Archie's as we see when they finally meet.

Each is also intimately linked to the Celtic culture around them, and because of these links, both are caught between worlds in the cultural changes overtaking the community. Archie's efforts to escape coal mining drive him into a world he can never understand, leaving him victim to the modern world's commodification of the human body. He has stumbled into a Darwinian world where dogs eat dogs. When he describes Archie's manager, MacLennan calls the reader's attention to his nose, or what's left of it. It has been bitten off in a fight, and only a stub remains, bearing the marks of his opponent's teeth. There is no emotion in the manager's actions; everything is merely business. His laugh, that of a eunuch, reveals the extent to which this world is controlled by half-men who may fear their prey but are ready to destroy those who come under their control. This impersonal cruelty strikes the reader as the key characteristic of modern culture, the culture toward which the uncomprehending Scots miners are being dragged.

On this same confusing battlefield, Dr. Ainslie also loses his way and just as perilously, although it is his soul and not his body that is in danger. If this were a movie, we might say that he is strongly attracted to the dark side in his rush to get what he wants. To do so, he will have to ruthlessly violate the autonomy of the two women who oppose him and, not incidentally, his own strong sense of moral justice. He plans his battles with amazing aplomb and with as little regard for others as his father had. But he is saved almost accidentally by his cultural connection to Mollie when he rediscovers his mother's heroic stature in Mollie's gallant and ferocious love, a love that he now realizes was embodied in his mother. Only now does he truly realize that, because of her love for her children, his mother was willing to die. In their encounters Mollie communicates without words but he understands, because they share so much. And what he understands is that in the face of her love and strength, no matter how great his malign desire, he is doomed to fail. This failure leads, paradoxically, to victory when he finally rejects, as his father did not, the destructive temptation of the patriarchal with its tainted goal. The loss of his "son" throws him into despair but after hurling his defiance at what seems to be his fate, he discovers that life can go on. He also discovers that life cannot be built on "no," his repeated cry to the indifferent universe. The acceptance of this void at the centre of his life comes, as it does in Carlyle's _Sartor Resartus_ , with a counterbalancing awareness that something does remain – his work. He now sees clearly what he has been trying to do to the people around him, and, in particular, the women he loves, and this shocks him into a recovery of his moral balance. Curiously, his response suggests that his Highland heritage is present in the strands of his better self. It is thus that he manages, for a moment anyway and with the help of his cultural roots, to integrate his past into his present.

Curiously, in so clearly masculine a book with its warrior-like preoccupations, the women, who emerge first as pale souls, are not ultimately destabilized by the chaos of change so prominent in the lives of their men. They are less vulnerable, perhaps because they already have a clear sense of what their lives are about. At first they seem to struggle to maintain their sense of self, but the reader soon discovers that both women are strong and willing to fight to have their voices heard. Like their husbands, there is much that they share. Each is sexual to her core and both are strongly attractive to the men around them. Mollie has a pale Celtic beauty that attracts the doctor and provides vivid memories for her husband. Margaret, the doctor's wife, has a voluptuousness that in Broughton sets her apart. The imagery associated with their desires suggests strongly that neither is caught in Calvinism's net (although we see Mollie's guilt over having had sex before marriage). They represent aspects of the eternal feminine, both lover and mother, and throughout the novel they preserve an autonomy that allows them to protect the ones they love. Mollie certainly acts to protect her son once she understands the doctor's intentions. When Ainslie confronts her, hoping to force her to acquiesce in his demand for Alan, it is she who wins. "There was a rock in her," he thinks, "she was stronger than he was" (184). She shows more such courage at the end when she steps between Archie and Louis Camire in a futile effort to bring an end to violence. Margaret is less sorely tried. She fulfills her role as wife, refusing to have servants about the place not because of cost but because she wants to be the centre of the home. However, her attempts to save her marriage seem doomed. She is continually rebuffed in her sexual overtures and finds it difficult even to persuade her husband to relax. Only at the end of the novel does she seem able to reach him because he has finally removed his emotional carapace as a result of his newly clarified love for Alan.

Each woman is distressed at her abandonment by her husband, but each refuses to allow her isolation to shake the values she holds. It helps that neither is caught, as the men are caught, in artificial systems that circumscribe their sense of self. They can neither resist nor deny the raw power possessed by their men, but they are willing to act at crucial moments in their own best interest and in the interest of those they love. At such moments both Molly and Margaret decisively intervene, Mollie by refusing to allow Alan to return to the doctor's house and Margaret by counselling Mollie to go to France with her lover. But because they cannot ultimately exert real control over their situation, they too are victims. Mollie MacNeil is killed for her infidelity, and Margaret Ainslie is placed in the nearly impossible position of having to welcome the boy she had earlier tried to keep out of her life even though she too, like her husband, has come to love him. What her future might be we cannot tell. Perhaps the doctor will learn this lesson as well and will finally return to her.

There are many ways in which the chaos produced by a clash of cultures must ultimately be destructive to the people caught up in it. As the chaos gives way to order, much that was important will have been lost. Thus the ending of the novel, with the dead bodies of the MacNeils like the aftermath of a miniature Culloden, proclaims the end of something important: there is no room for the old Highland Scottish cultural ideals in the industrial age in Cape Breton. MacLennan oversees their destruction rather in the manner of the British general Wade, who in the eighteenth century reduced the Highland clans by destroying their isolation, building roads that brought not only the British Army to their doorstep but new ideas as well. In so doing, Wade ended the old, agrarian life of the Scottish Highland warrior. MacLennan might mourn the disappearance of the poetry implicit in the image of the Highland warrior, but he is certain that the Highland Scots of Cape Breton, like their ancestors, must put aside their traditional ways. Archie's fate, tragic in its own right, parallels that of the original Highlanders, for he too has been "lost in the lowlands of the shrewd men" (98). The darling of a people, he incarnated the image of the warrior of ancient days, carrying their hopes with him, only to be destroyed by a system imposed by the "shrewd men," a fate all the more affecting because the author has given us such harrowing scenes of his downfall. Archie says little but he is surrounded by such vivid images, from his tender recall of his first night with Mollie to those that map the trajectory of his downfall, that we understand his importance to the author and the novel. Dr. Ainslie's pain can sometimes seem remote but Archie's physical demolition is overwhelming in its immediacy. He is, like all of us, caught up in a world he does not understand. His tragedy is not Aristotelian, but it does encapsulate more modern fears than we care to acknowledge. Yet from MacLennan's point of view it was necessary not only that Archie be destroyed but that the image of the warrior be destroyed as well. Archie's death tells us clearly that this is no longer a world of warriors, and that, like Billy Blackett, the fingerless Newfoundlander, the Highlanders must learn new ways of doing things, ways that use their heads and not their fists or swords – or shovels for that matter, since the mines are as deadly and destructive as the old ideals. But it is also a world from which much that was good has vanished: the power of one's own language and one's communal music, the nearness to one's roots and one's own stories, and the comfort of the shared experience of a large extended family. For the ordinary folk of the novel, the despair of the novel's ending betrays the inevitable loss of a culture, only scraps of which can be rescued. Some, like Angus the Barraman, have not lost everything and can step forward to help others as their culture has taught them. The value of this culture is not diminished by MacLennan's determined refusal to recognize its validity.

But the final chapters also signal a beginning that for MacLennan is at the thematic centre of the novel. Daniel Ainslie's final victory, achieved with such pain, destroys his father's hold over his life and spirit and so allows him to escape the ancient Calvinist curse. He can now leave behind that part of his past and build for the future in the knowledge that other foundations can serve as the basis of a man's life. His tears, born of love, in the last few chapters put a seal on his transformation. The emotional constraints of his earlier persona have finally given way as his earlier truculence dissolves. His victory over his father's heritage comes at great cost and he will not escape his responsibility for earlier wishing to embrace his paternal heritage. Nor can he leave behind the guilt that is an acknowledgment of his own part in the tragedy through his manipulation of others and his attempted violation of their autonomy. But the end of the curse that had dogged him allows him to look to the future with a wife who loves him and whom he may now love in every sense, and to the presence of a son, reborn (as the symbolism suggests), even if in horror and baptized in blood. Ainslie's assumption of parenthood signals a world forever changed, or perhaps one world gained for another world lost. But in the end his great victory is not simply the loss of his Calvinist fears but an acceptance, even if unrecognized, of the need to be the ideal Highland warrior who protects those who depend upon him, putting the well-being of others before his own. Perhaps he will think better of his focus on the perilous virtues of autonomy and come to see how strength as well as weakness can be the legacy of our collective past. We are given no glimpse into his future except his intention to pursue his studies abroad, although the reader might be forgiven for thinking it dark and uncertain. But it will at least be based on a strong, selfless love that satisfies the doctor's needs like nothing else.

Curses, says MacLennan, are not eternal. We can all be transformed and made ready to welcome a new future. And yet it is clear that the muted nature of the conclusion speaks of other considerations as well. While the novel is the record of a victory over the malign influence of some religious dogmas, it is also about defeat, the defeat not only of a man but of a culture. The climactic scene, in an appropriation of Cape Breton's future, removes Alan from his cultural roots and delivers him willy-nilly into the orbit of Oxford University – interestingly, the same dream that MacLennan's father cherished for him. This brutal dismissal of local culture as mere "colour" is not the last word, however, and may paradoxically help the reader realize the wealth that lies in that tradition. Remnants of old Cape Breton may be dismissed by the author but they can provoke us to think about cultural change generally, even if we are forced to accept inevitability. It seems that the modern world will always win such battles and that people, as they mourn the old (as inevitably they must), can only try to save what they can. It is also clear that the setting for such cultural collisions is not only to be found on Cape Breton's shores but on any territory where such contradictory forces come into play, and that a similar story may and no doubt will be told of many peoples in ages yet to come.

NOTES

1 The original home of the MacLennans, Kintail was mountainous and barren. A few sheep might have survived on the slopes, and the hardy highland cattle were the main export by the early nineteenth century. In the late eighteenth and early nineteenth centuries, these would be driven in herds over mountainous terrain, Swiss-like as a friend from the Isle of Skye describes it. These herds eventually made their way to the distant south, to Glasgow or Falkirk, where they were sold into a beef market that had earlier offered good prices but that bottomed out in the early 1830s. It was no doubt easy to pick up information on these visits about the opportunities in the New World, into whose expanses waves of Scots had already been absorbed. Archives offer us examples of letters sent home by the earliest migrants extolling the virtues of the New World, and their information was no doubt spread by word of mouth whenever such a letter arrived. There was, said the letters, land to be had, and cheaply. Whatever the immediate reasons might have been, the forced clearances of the land by greedy landlords or the fall in the price of goods following the Napoleonic wars, one can easily see the rationale for emigration.
